### Question 1: I am writing to request your review of my course tracking in preparation for graduation. I am pursuing a double major in Computer Science and Applied Mathematics. I have attached the spreadsheet tracking my courses for your review. I would appreciate it if you could take a look and provide any necessary feedback: Humility, Global Humanities and Social Change, Linear Algebra, Modern Vietnamese Culture and Society, Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age, Discrete Math, Computer Science II: Data Structures, Programming for Data Science and Visualization, Design and Systems Thinking, Scientific Inquiry, Computer Organization, Calculus, Intelligent Robot Studio: From Theory to Practice, How to become a writer: lessons from Vietnamese and non-Vietnamese writers, Introduction to Political Science, The Global Vietnam War II, Deep Learning for Artificial Intelligence, Advanced Deep Learning, Probability, Computer Science I: Introduction to Programming, Algorithms and Theory of Computing, Database System, Advanced Programming Techniques, Introduction to Artificial Intelligence, Machine Learning for Data Science, Multivariable Calculus, Mathematical Statistics, General Work Program (GWP) 1, [Independent Research Project] Labeled FreSCo- Mining Frequent Patterns in labeled Simplicial Complexes, Object-Oriented Analysis and Design, Statistical Learning, Operating Systems, Abstract Algebra, Differential Equations, Stochastic Calculus, Natural Language Processing, Advanced Linear Algebra.  
### Answer: 

The course tracking you've provided shows a comprehensive and rigorous academic journey towards a double major in Computer Science and Applied Mathematics. Here are some observations and suggestions for your consideration:

1. **Core Curriculum Completion**: You've completed all the core curriculum requirements, which is a solid foundation for your studies. Ensure that you've met all the specific course requirements, such as passing the Global Humanities and Modern Vietnamese Culture and Society courses.

2. **Balance Between Theoretical and Applied Courses**: Your course selection demonstrates a good balance between theoretical mathematics courses (e.g., Linear Algebra, Calculus, Probability, Abstract Algebra) and applied mathematics and computer science courses (e.g., Programming for Data Science and Visualization, Machine Learning for Data Science, Computer Science I & II). This balance is crucial for a strong understanding of both the foundational principles and practical applications of your chosen fields.

3. **Elective Courses**: The selection of elective courses, such as "How to become a writer: lessons from Vietnamese and non-Vietnamese writers" and "The Global Vietnam War II," shows a broad and interdisciplinary approach to your education. While these courses may not be directly related to your majors, they contribute to a well-rounded education and can enhance your critical thinking and communication skills.

4. **Research and Capstone Project**: Your participation in an Independent Research Project titled "FreSCo- Mining Frequent Patterns in labeled Simplicial Complexes" is commendable. This project not only demonstrates your commitment to applied mathematics but also your ability to engage in independent research. Ensure that this project aligns with the requirements of your degree program and that you have the necessary support and resources to complete it successfully.

5. **Graduation Requirements**: Double majoring can be challenging, but you seem to be on track to meet all the necessary requirements for graduation. Keep an eye on any specific course sequences or prerequisites that might be required for both majors.

6. **Career Readiness**: With your chosen majors, you are well-positioned for careers in data science, artificial intelligence, software development, and related fields. Consider seeking internships or co-op opportunities to gain practical experience and build your professional network.

7. **Continued Learning and Development**: Keep abreast of the latest developments in your fields of study, especially in data science and artificial intelligence. These fields evolve rapidly, and continuous learning will be key to your professional success.

In summary, your course tracking indicates a strong and well-rounded academic plan. Continue to focus on meeting all degree requirements, seek opportunities for practical experience, and stay engaged with the latest advancements in your fields of study. If you have any specific concerns or questions about your course selection or progression towards graduation, don't hesitate to reach out to your academic advisors for further guidance.

### Question 2: Can you differentiate a cross-listed course and elective applied course in the case of a double major?  
### Answer: 

Cross-listed courses are those that are shared between two different majors (or a major and a minor) and can be used to satisfy the requirements of both up to a limit of 8 credits (two courses). This means that if a course is cross-listed between major A and major B, it can count towards the requirements for both majors, up to a total of 8 credits. 

On the other hand, elective applied courses are specific courses from other majors that emphasize the applications of Mathematics. These courses are not necessarily shared between majors but are chosen by the student to supplement their major requirements. They must be at least 2 in number, with at least one at the 200 level, and can include subjects like Game Theory from Economics, Signal Systems and Control from Engineering, or Introduction to Machine Learning from Computer Science.

In the case of a double major, cross-listed courses can be strategically chosen to reduce the total number of courses a student needs to take, up to the 8-credit limit per pair of majors. Elective applied courses, however, are additional courses chosen by the student to enhance their mathematical skills and are not constrained by the cross-listing rule. They must be taken for a letter grade and cannot be double-counted for two different requirements.

To differentiate:

- Cross-listed courses: Shared between majors, can be double-counted up to 8 credits.
- Elective applied courses: From other majors, chosen by the student to supplement their major, not double-counted for different requirements.

In the context of a double major, cross-listed courses are a tool to streamline the curriculum and reduce redundancy, while elective applied courses are additional, chosen electives that help the student gain a broader perspective and deeper understanding of applied mathematics.

and a minor) share cross- listed or tagged1 courses, up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor) and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. 1 In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors. ### Question 3: I would like to ask for the capstone withdrawal policy. What will be the impact when I choose to drop the capstone before the Fall 2025 term starts? Will there be any penalties associated with withdrawing, such as a 'W' notation on my transcript?  
### Answer: 

The policy for withdrawing from a course, including a capstone course, is clearly outlined in the provided context. According to the policy, if you choose to withdraw from a course before the halfway point of the term (end of week 4 for an 8-week course or end of week 8 for a full-term course), you can do so without any consequence on your academic record. Specifically, from Week 3 to Week 4 of the term, you can withdraw without any consequences in the Academic Records. 

This means that if you withdraw from the capstone course during this period (Week 3 to Week 4 of the Fall 2025 term), there will be no 'W' notation on your transcript, and it will not affect your GPA. However, if you choose to withdraw after Week 4, attempted credits and a 'W' grade will be recorded on your transcript, which does not affect your GPA.

It is important to note that you should consult with your Academic/Major Advisor before withdrawing from the course to ensure you are making the best decision for your academic progress and to understand any potential implications for your degree program.

Additionally, the policy mentions that withdrawing from a course beyond the specified period will result in an 'F' grade, which will affect your GPA, unless there are exceptional circumstances. In such cases, you would need to provide supporting documentation and receive authorization from the course instructor(s), your academic/major advisor, and the Registrar’s Office.

Therefore, the key takeaway is that if you withdraw from the capstone course before the end of Week 4 of the Fall 2025 term, there will be no 'W' notation on your transcript and no impact on your GPA. However, if you withdraw after Week 4, a 'W' grade will be recorded, and if you withdraw after the halfway point without exceptional circumstances, an 'F' grade will be recorded, affecting your GPA.

Regarding penalties, there are no penalties in terms of a 'W' notation or GPA impact if you withdraw before the halfway point of the term. However, withdrawing after the halfway point without exceptional circumstances will result in an 'F' grade, which is a penalty in terms of GPA.

The Fees and Charges Guide should be consulted for any related fees associated with the withdrawal process.

In summary, the withdrawal policy is designed to allow students to adjust their course load without severe academic consequences early in the term, but students should be mindful of the deadlines and consult with their advisors to make informed decisions.

### Question 4: Questions related to course history, in which one course can have multiple IDs? Like Advanced Deep Learning course. How would it be handled among different cohorts?  
### Answer: 

The Advanced Deep Learning course, referred to as CS311, is offered once a year. It is designed to provide students with a deeper understanding of deep learning techniques and their applications. The course ID (CS311) remains consistent across different cohorts, allowing for continuity and standardization in course offerings. However, the specific content, examples, and case studies might be updated to reflect the latest advancements and trends in the field of deep learning.

The course description and prerequisites are clearly defined, indicating that students should have completed CS201 and ideally have some background in mathematics (MATH103 is suggested). This structure ensures that students have the foundational knowledge necessary to engage with the advanced topics covered in the course.

The course is part of the Computer Science curriculum, which is structured to provide students with a comprehensive education in computer science fundamentals, intermediate concepts, and specialized advanced topics. Students are expected to build on their knowledge progressively, starting with foundational courses like Introduction to Programming (CS101), Data Structures & Algorithms, and so on, before moving on to intermediate courses such as Operating Systems (CS211) and Computer Networks (CS210).

After completing the required foundational and intermediate courses, students have the flexibility to choose four elective advanced courses, including CS311 Advanced Deep Learning, to specialize in their areas of interest. This allows for a tailored educational experience that can prepare students for various career paths within the field of computer science and artificial intelligence.

In terms of capstone experiences, students have the option to either complete a capstone project to graduate with honors or take additional courses to deepen their knowledge without the honors distinction. This structure provides a clear pathway for students to achieve their academic and career goals while ensuring they have a strong foundation in computer science principles and advanced skills in specialized areas like deep learning.

The course descriptions and curriculum design reflect a commitment to staying current with technological advancements and industry needs, ensuring that students are well-prepared for the challenges and opportunities in the field of computer science and artificial intelligence. The consistency in course IDs and the structured progression of courses help maintain a coherent and accessible educational experience for all students, regardless of their cohort.

### Question 5: I have taken Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis in Fall 2023. Am I eligible to take Financial Economics in Spring 2024?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you are eligible to take Financial Economics in Spring 2024. According to the provided major outline, students should complete all Intermediate level courses (Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis) by the end of their third year before taking Advanced level courses. Since you have completed these Intermediate level courses, you are now eligible to take Advanced level courses such as Financial Economics in your fourth year. However, it's always a good idea to check with the Economics major coordinator to ensure that all prerequisites are met and to confirm any updates to the curriculum.

Courses: Principles of Economics 1 and 2 (100-level) These two courses introduce microeconomics and macroeconomics, providing an overview of economic theories and analytical techniques applied to current issues. By studying both microeconomics (the analysis of choices made by people, firms, and governments) and macroeconomics (the analysis of the economy as a whole) students will develop their economic intuition as they better understand the functioning of the world. Methodology Courses: Economic Methods (100-level) This course introduces basic concepts and methods of statistical analysis and fundamental rules of mathematics used in economic analysis. Students will be exposed to a wide range of economic problems and the methods to solve them. By completing this course, students will be qualified to take upper-level courses in the Economics major. ### Question 6: If I have waived the course Computer Science I, but I want to declare Computer Science major, do I have to take Computer Science I?  
### Answer: 

To formally declare Computer Science as their major, the student must complete at least two Computer Science courses (MATH 202 - Discrete Math is also counted). Since you have waived Computer Science I, you still need to take at least one other Computer Science course to meet the requirement of having completed two Computer Science courses. Therefore, you would need to choose from the other available Computer Science courses to fulfill this requirement. The specific courses you can take depend on the curriculum and your interests within the field of Computer Science.

### Question 7: If I have taken the following courses: Mathematical Statistics, Abstract Algebra, Linear Algebra, Multivariable Calculus, Calculus, Differential Equation, Stochastic Calculus, Statistical Learning, Discrete Mathematics, Probability, am I eligible to declare a minor in Applied Mathematics? What about majoring in Applied Mathematics?  
### Answer: 

To declare a minor in Applied Mathematics, you would typically need to complete at least 6 Mathematics courses, which include at least 1 Advanced course and 2 Intermediate courses. Based on the courses you've listed:

- **Foundation Courses**: You've completed Calculus and Linear Algebra, which are typically considered Foundation courses.
- **Intermediate Courses**: You've completed Differential Equations, Probability, Discrete Mathematics, and Statistical Learning, which are all Intermediate level courses.
- **Advanced Courses**: You've completed Mathematical Statistics, Stochastic Calculus, and Statistical Learning, which are Advanced courses.

Given this, you have completed the requirements for the minor in Applied Mathematics, as you have:

- At least 2 Intermediate courses (Differential Equations, Probability)
- At least 1 Advanced course (Mathematical Statistics, Stochastic Calculus, or Statistical Learning)

You have more than enough courses to satisfy the minor requirements. 

For **majoring** in Applied Mathematics, the requirements are more extensive:

- At least 2 Foundation courses (you've completed these)
- At least 2 Intermediate courses (you've completed more than the required 2)
- At least 2 Advanced courses (you've completed more than the required 2)
- Completion of a Capstone project (optional but recommended for honors)

Since you've already completed more than the minimum required courses for both the minor and the major, you are eligible to declare a minor in Applied Mathematics. For majoring in Applied Mathematics, you would need to ensure you complete the Capstone project and possibly additional courses as advised by your academic advisor to meet all the major's specific requirements.

**Note**: Always consult with your academic advisor to ensure you're meeting all the specific requirements of your institution, as course titles and requirements can vary between different universities.

### Question 8: I have completed these 100-level courses: Introduction to Art History and Theory, Introduction to Film History and Theory. Am I eligible to start a Capstone in my major Arts and Media Studies?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you are eligible to start a Capstone in Arts and Media Studies based on the eligibility criteria provided. You have completed the necessary 100-level major requirements (Introduction to Art History and Theory, Introduction to Film History and Theory), and you must also meet the other criteria such as having a major GPA of 3.6 and no outstanding Incompletes at the time of application. It is also important to consult early with your prospective advisor to ensure that your proposed capstone project is within the appropriate scope and that there is faculty expertise available to support your project.

### Question 9: Is it possible that I write a book as my History Capstone?  
### Answer: 

Based on the provided context, the History Capstone project guidelines do not explicitly mention writing a book as an acceptable format. The suggested alternative formats include a short documentary film, a podcast, a historical exhibition, or an interactive digital narrative. While a book could certainly be considered a substantial written component, the guidelines specifically mention a "written thesis" and "other formats" that are more explicitly defined.

The context suggests that a traditional thesis is the most common format, and it implies that students might choose from a list of alternative formats that are more project-based and interactive. Writing a full-length book would likely fall outside of these suggested formats, as it is not explicitly listed.

However, the context also states that there is no specific format requirement, and students are free to adopt other formats as long as they have a substantial written component. If you are interested in writing a book, you would need to propose this format to your advisor and potentially the department for approval, as it is not one of the explicitly mentioned options.

In summary, while it is technically possible to write a book as your History Capstone, it would not be in line with the suggested formats provided in the guidelines. You would need to discuss this option with your advisor and potentially seek departmental approval to pursue such a project.

### Question 10: Given I have finished these subjects: Advanced Deep Learning, Machine Learning, Deep Learning, Database. Are they sufficient for the Advanced Courses requirement of the computer science major?  
### Answer: 

The courses you've completed cover a broad range of topics in computer science, including machine learning, deep learning, and advanced deep learning. These are crucial for the Advanced Courses requirement of a computer science major, as they provide a strong foundation in both theoretical and practical aspects of modern machine learning and deep learning techniques.

Machine Learning (CS307) covers fundamental concepts and algorithms in supervised and unsupervised learning, reinforcement learning, and general techniques. This course is essential for understanding the principles behind various machine learning models and methods.

Deep Learning for AI (CS313) and Advanced Deep Learning (CS311) build upon the foundational knowledge from CS307, focusing on advanced architectures of deep neural networks, algorithms, and practical applications in various domains such as economics, fintech, computer vision, and natural language processing. These courses are particularly relevant for the advanced courses requirement, as they delve into the latest research and applications in deep learning.

Database (which is not explicitly listed in the prerequisites but is generally a core component of a computer science curriculum) is also important for understanding data management, storage, and retrieval, which are critical for building and deploying machine learning and deep learning systems.

While these courses provide a strong foundation in machine learning, deep learning, and data management, it's always beneficial to keep learning and stay updated with the latest developments in the field. Additionally, depending on the specific requirements of your computer science major, you might also consider courses in related areas such as artificial intelligence, natural language processing, computer vision, and specialized electives that align with your career interests.

In summary, the courses you've completed are indeed sufficient for the Advanced Courses requirement of a computer science major, especially if your program emphasizes machine learning and deep learning. However, always check with your academic advisor to ensure you meet all the specific requirements of your degree program.

### Question 11: I have a question if I take a gap year, will my financial aid be canceled? If yes, can I still re-apply for the financial aid program when I get back to school?  
### Answer: 

Students who are re-admitted must also re- apply for financial aid. So yes, if you take a gap year, your financial aid will be canceled. When you return to school, you can re-apply for the financial aid program. However, you must meet the new criteria and requirements for financial aid, as they may have changed since your last application. It is important to contact the Financial Aid Office at your institution to discuss your specific situation and to get detailed information on the re-application process.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 11]
student must meet with the Dean’s Office, who establishes the conditions that the student must meet prior to returning to Fulbright. While on suspension, a student may not visit the Fulbright campus unless permission is given from the Dean’s Office or the Vice President of Student Affairs for the purpose of attending relevant meetings. Suspensions typically are for one term or one academic year. A student may be suspended a maximum of two times. A student will not be suspended a third time but will instead have their enrolment in the program canceled. A student in this situation must apply for re-admission. The student must include with his or her application for re-admission a letter that addresses the reasons for their suspensions and cancellation, and the measures they have taken to address those reasons. The re-admission decision is made by the Dean’s Office in consultation with the appropriate members of faculty and staff. Students who are re-admitted must also re- apply for financial aid.

### Question 12: I am aware that Fulbright has to organize online classes instead of on-campus ones. Therefore, will there be any change in the tuition fee?  
### Answer: 

There is no mention of any change in tuition fees due to the mode of course delivery, whether online or on-campus. The tuition fee policies and procedures at Fulbright, as outlined in the provided contexts, do not specify any adjustments based on the delivery method of courses. The tuition fee structure and policies, including those related to course withdrawals and waitlists, remain consistent and are governed by the “Fees & Charges Guide” for undergraduate students. Therefore, the tuition fee remains unchanged regardless of whether courses are delivered online or on-campus. The contexts focus on course registration, withdrawal, and waitlist policies, and do not address any changes in tuition fees due to the current circumstances of organizing online classes instead of on-campus ones.

### Question 13: If I major in Economics, is it sufficient that I take Scholar Development to fulfill Experiential Learning requirements?  
### Answer: 

The context provided outlines the requirements for earning a Bachelor of Arts in Economics at Fulbright University. Specifically, it mentions that Experiential Learning (EL) can count toward elective or major requirements, but it must be approved by an advisor. The university's Experiential Learning Program (ELP) offers various streams, including those housed within Academic Affairs (AA), Career Services (CS), and the Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation, and all full-time, undergraduate students must complete at least 4 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement.

Regarding your question about whether Scholar Development can fulfill the Experiential Learning requirements for an Economics major, the answer is that it depends on the specific program and approval process at Fulbright University. Scholar Development could potentially be an approved experiential learning activity if it meets the criteria set by the university for experiential learning. However, the university's policy states that the ELP must be approved by an advisor, so you would need to discuss with your academic advisor to determine if Scholar Development qualifies and to get the necessary approval.

It's important to note that while Scholar Development might offer valuable learning experiences, it may not necessarily align with all the streams offered by the ELP (such as those in AA, CS, or the Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation). Therefore, it's crucial to have a conversation with your advisor to ensure that the chosen activity aligns with the university's expectations for experiential learning and will be appropriately credited toward your degree requirements.

In summary, while Scholar Development could potentially fulfill the Experiential Learning requirement, it is contingent upon approval from your academic advisor and alignment with the university's criteria for experiential learning activities.

### Question 14: Is it sufficient for me to complete 4 credits in EL if I major in Engineering?  
### Answer: 

The degree requirements for an engineering major include Experiential Learning (EL) which must be between 4 to 12 credits. Completing the minimum of 4 credits in EL is sufficient to meet the requirement, but it is important to note that at least 1 of those credits must be Scholar Development, which must be approved by the Chair of Engineering. However, it is generally recommended to take more than the minimum credits in EL to gain a broader and more in-depth experience, which can be beneficial for your career and further studies. Additionally, the engineering curriculum is designed to progressively build upon foundational knowledge and skills, with a focus on reinforcing and practicing these skills at the intermediate and advanced levels. The Capstone courses, in particular, are crucial for applying and integrating the knowledge and skills acquired throughout the program. Therefore, while 4 credits in EL is the minimum requirement, striving for a richer and more comprehensive educational experience by taking the full range (4-12 credits) is advisable.

### Question 15: Can I take all four engineering foundation courses and only two intermediate engineering courses?  
### Answer: 

Yes, according to the degree requirements, students can take all four engineering foundation courses (16 credits) and only two intermediate engineering courses (8 credits) as part of their Exploratory courses. However, it's important to note that the degree requirements also specify that students must complete a total of 8 Exploratory courses (32 credits), and up to two of these courses (8 credits) can be counted towards the major. Therefore, while you can take the specified number of foundation and intermediate courses, you must ensure that your total Exploratory course credits meet the 32-credit requirement, and that any courses counted towards the major are appropriately chosen based on your future specialization within engineering. Additionally, you must also fulfill the other degree requirements, such as completing the core courses, mathematics or basic science courses, a programming course, and the Capstone project or an additional engineering course.

### Question 16: If I have completed the course Intro to AI, will CS207 - Object-Oriented Analysis and Design and CS211 – Operating Systems suffice to fill the rest two intermediate courses?  
### Answer: 

CS207 - Object-Oriented Analysis and Design and CS211 – Operating Systems are both intermediate courses in computer science. Therefore, they would suffice to fill the rest two intermediate courses requirement after completing the Intro to AI course. However, it's important to note that the specific requirements for intermediate courses might vary depending on the university or program. Always check with your academic advisor to ensure that the courses you choose meet the specific requirements of your degree program. Additionally, while CS207 focuses on object-oriented analysis and design, and CS211 covers operating systems, they complement each other and provide a broad understanding of computer science concepts. However, they might not directly cover all topics that could be included in an AI curriculum, so it's essential to ensure that your overall course selection aligns with your academic goals and program requirements.

(AI) is a research ﬁeld that studies how to realize the intelligent human behaviors on a computer. The ultimate goal of AI is to make a computer that can learn, plan, and solve problems autonomously. ### Question 17: Does completing both Microeconomics and Macroeconomics satisfy the core theory requirement, or is an additional course needed?  
### Answer: 

Completing both Microeconomics and Macroeconomics satisfies the core theory requirement. According to the provided degree requirements, students must complete two analysis courses (8 credits), which are Microeconomic Analysis and Macroeconomic Analysis. These two courses together fulfill the core theory requirement for the Economics major. Therefore, no additional course is needed beyond these two to satisfy the core theory requirement.

### Question 18: If I want to major in Engineering, can I choose other programming courses besides Computer Science I and Computer Science II, for instance Introduction to data visualization?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can choose other programming courses besides Computer Science I and Computer Science II if you want to major in Engineering. The course "Introduction to Data Visualization" (CS103) is specifically designed to provide students with a set of skills to analyze the nature of data across different domains. It demonstrates data visualization by understanding, questioning, and problematizing how data are generated, analyzed, and used. The course also familiarizes students with Python programming language and its libraries to analyze, visualize, and discover data insights leading to proper data-driven decision-making processes.

The course alignment with the Program Learning Outcomes (PLOs) indicates that it covers essential aspects of computer science, such as understanding fundamental concepts (PLO1) and applying knowledge to real-world applications (PLO3), which are relevant for an Engineering major. While CS101 (Introduction to Programming) and CS104 (Introduction to Computer Science I) are foundational courses, CS103 can complement your education by providing valuable data science and visualization skills.

However, it's important to check the specific requirements of your Engineering program, as some programs may have specific course prerequisites or recommendations. Always consult with your academic advisor to ensure that your course selection aligns with your major requirements and overall educational goals.

### Question 19: Given I took Discrete Mathematics in Spring 2022 and earned B, then the same course in Spring 2024 and earned C-, which grades will be kept?  
### Answer: 

The higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript.

### Question 20: Can I register 300-level math courses as exploratory courses E4?  
### Answer: 

No, the Exploratory requirements specify that students must take courses at the 100-level or 200-level. The context states that students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses) from each of the three categories (E1, E2, and STEM), and these courses must be at the 100-level or 200-level. The mention of 300-level math courses is irrelevant to fulfilling the Exploratory requirements because the requirements explicitly restrict courses to the lower-level classifications. Therefore, 300-level math courses cannot be registered as Exploratory courses E4.

### Question 21: Can I register for more than 20 credits in a semester?  
### Answer: 

No, according to the given context, students are only allowed to register for a maximum course load of 20 credit hours per term. The form titled "[F02 – RO/FUV]: Request for maximum course load per term" is specifically designed for students who wish to register for this maximum load, and it requires the student to complete the form, get the Academic Advisor's approval, and submit it to the Office of the Registrar before the end date of the online registration period. Therefore, registering for more than 20 credits in a semester is not permitted based on the information provided. The references to Exploratory and major requirements, as well as the rules about double majors and cross-listed courses, are irrelevant to the question of maximum course load per term.

### Question 22: What is the maximum number of times I can retake a course to improve my grade, and how does it affect my transcript?  
### Answer: 

The maximum number of times a student can retake a course is twice, unless they failed the second time or need a specific grade to meet major requirements. If a student repeats a course, the higher grade is kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade is overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remains on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. A student may not retake the same course for the third time unless they failed the second time or if a specific grade is needed to meet the major requirements. Students shall consult with their Academic/Major Advisor about their program plan if they are considering taking the class for a third time.

Students shall consult with their Academic/Major Advisor about their program plan if they are considering taking the class for a third time. 4.2 Final Grade Release Final grades will be posted by the Registrar’s Office and accessible via the One Stop portal. 4.3 Credit Transfer and Exemptions earned from other universities. ### Question 23: For a student doing the literature capstone, if a student includes charts or tables, do they count toward the word limit?  
### Answer: 

The guidelines specifically mention that the final submission should be 8,000-10,000 words, including endnotes or footnotes, but not including full bibliography and images with captions. This implies that charts or tables are not included in the word count. The guidelines clearly state that only the textual or written component is to be counted towards the word limit, and explicitly exclude full bibliography and images with captions. Therefore, charts or tables would not count towards the word limit.

### Question 24: If I failed Capstone I in the Fall 2024, can I redo it in Spring 2025?  
### Answer: 

Based on the provided contexts, if you failed Capstone I in the Fall 2024, you would not automatically be allowed to redo it in the Spring 2025 semester without further action. According to the guidelines, students who fail to satisfactorily complete Capstone I are subject to a "NO Pass" and are recommended not to proceed with Capstone II. The decision to allow a student to repeat Capstone I would likely be at the discretion of the faculty and would depend on several factors:

1. **Faculty Review**: The major faculty would assess the reasons for the failure and may decide whether a redo is appropriate.

2. **Capstone Coordinator**: The major Capstone coordinator might also be involved in the decision-making process, especially if there are workload distribution issues or other administrative considerations.

3. **Advisors' Input**: Your primary and secondary advisors would likely be consulted regarding your performance and potential for improvement.

4. **GPA Requirement**: Since maintaining a 3.2 GPA throughout the senior year is required to earn Honors for the Capstone, your overall academic performance would also be a consideration.

5. **Capstone Handbook**: The History Capstone Handbook, AY2025-26, does not explicitly state a policy for repeating Capstone I, but it does outline the consequences of failure and the importance of active participation and quality of submission.

In summary, while the handbook does not explicitly state a policy for repeating Capstone I, it is likely that a student who fails would need to petition the faculty or Capstone coordinator for permission to repeat the course. This would typically involve demonstrating a clear understanding of the issues that led to the failure and a plan for improvement. The decision would be made on a case-by-case basis, considering the student's academic record, the reasons for the failure, and the faculty's workload and capacity.

It is important to note that the use of generative AI in the Capstone project must also adhere to the Code of Academic Integrity, which prohibits presenting content created by AI as one's own work. Any use of AI tools must be disclosed and appropriately cited in accordance with academic standards.

In conclusion, while the handbook does not explicitly state a policy for repeating Capstone I, it is likely that a student would need to seek permission from the faculty or Capstone coordinator to repeat the course after failing it. This would involve demonstrating a clear understanding of the issues that led to the failure and a plan for improvement. The decision would be made on a case-by-case basis, considering various factors including the student's academic record and the faculty's workload and capacity.

### Question 25: If I do Literature capstone in Decolonial studies, can it be approved?  
### Answer: 

The Literature capstone project can be approved if it aligns with the expertise of the current full-time faculty in the relevant fields. According to the given context, the faculty in Literature hold expertise in areas such as modern Vietnamese literature and history, literature of the environment, early modern and contemporary poetry, Asian diasporic literature, creative writing, translation, gender and sexuality studies, postcolonial studies, and medieval, Renaissance, and classical studies.

Decolonial studies, as a field, intersects with several of these areas, particularly gender and sexuality studies, postcolonial studies, and possibly modern Vietnamese literature and history. Therefore, if a student proposes a capstone project in Decolonial studies that falls within these areas of expertise, it is likely to be approved.

However, it is crucial for the student to consult with prospective advisor(s) early in their junior year to discuss the suitability and feasibility of the proposed project. This consultation will help ensure that the project scope is appropriate and that the student has the necessary support and resources to complete the project successfully.

Moreover, the project must demonstrate competency in a primary disciplinary specialization, optionally supported by secondary and tertiary disciplinary areas. Given the interdisciplinary nature of Decolonial studies, the student would need to clearly articulate how their project fits within the Literature department's expertise and how it contributes to the field.

In summary, a Literature capstone in Decolonial studies can be approved if it aligns with the faculty's expertise, is appropriately scoped, and receives the support of a qualified advisor. The student should ensure they have a clear plan and are prepared to discuss their project's relevance and feasibility with the faculty.

### Question 26: I am a Co24 majoring in Computer Science and taking the Database course. Does it count as major courses?  
### Answer: 

Yes, the Database Systems course (CS304) is listed as a course that is offered once a year and is part of the Computer Science major curriculum. Specifically, it is listed under the "Once a year CS201" availability, which means it is available to students who have completed CS201 Data Structures & Algorithms. Since you are a Computer Science major (Co24) and you are taking this course, it definitely counts as a major course. It contributes to the requirement of taking four advanced courses (16 credits) as part of your major requirements.

### Question 27: Can Discrete Mathematics be counted for both Computer Science and Applied Mathematics majors?  
### Answer: 

Yes, Discrete Mathematics can be counted for both Computer Science and Applied Mathematics majors. In the context provided, MATH 202: Discrete Math is specifically mentioned as a required course for the Computer Science major, and it is also implied that it can be counted towards the requirements for the Applied Mathematics major, as it is listed under the foundation courses. The course is essential for developing logical reasoning and formal proof skills, which are crucial for both fields. Additionally, the course's content, which includes topics such as combinatorics, graph theory, and number theory, is fundamental to both the theoretical underpinnings and practical applications in computer science and applied mathematics. Therefore, Discrete Mathematics serves as a bridge between the two disciplines, providing a strong foundation in mathematical thinking and problem-solving that is applicable across a wide range of intellectual domains.

Demonstrate skills to communicate mathematical ideas orally and in writing, with precision, clarity and organization, using proper terminology and notation. 7. Develop efficient computer algorithms and programs for quantitative analysis and mathematical modeling. 8. Develop necessary skills to work well in a multidisciplinary team. ### Question 28: What steps should I take to ensure a smooth transfer of my exchange semester credits from Bard College to Fulbright?  
### Answer: 

To ensure a smooth transfer of your exchange semester credits from Bard College to Fulbright University Vietnam, follow these steps:

1. **Read the Transfer Credit Policy**: Before submitting any requests, ensure you have read and understood the Transfer Credit Policy V1.0 published in the One-Stop portal. This will give you a clear understanding of the requirements and procedures.

2. **Gather Required Information**: Compile all the necessary information about the Bard College courses you wish to transfer. This includes but is not limited to course curriculum, learning outcomes, course content, and assessments.

3. **Complete the Application for Credit Transfer**: Fill out the Application for Credit Transfer form provided by Fulbright University Vietnam. Make sure to include all the requested information about the courses you are applying to transfer.

4. **Submit the Application and Official Transcript**: Along with the completed application form, send an official transcript with a seal from Bard College directly to the Office of the Registrar at registrar@fulbright.edu.vn or the mailing address specified in the Transfer Credit Policy.

5. **Declaration**: Ensure that you sign the declaration section of the application form, affirming that the information provided is complete and correct.

6. **Follow Up**: After submitting your application and documents, follow up with the Office of the Registrar to ensure they have received everything and to inquire about the status of your transfer credit evaluation.

7. **Coordinate with Your Academic Advisor**: Keep your academic advisor informed about your transfer credit application process. They may be able to provide additional support or guidance.

8. **Be Prepared for Evaluation**: Fulbright will determine transferable credit after you enroll. Be prepared for the possibility that not all of your credits may be transferred, and be flexible in adjusting your academic plan accordingly.

9. **Check the AP/IB/CIE Credit Policy**: If you have taken A-level, AP, IB, or CIE courses, be aware that the transfer credit for these will be determined according to the AP/IB/CIE Credit Policy (in development), which you can find in the One Stop portal.

By following these steps, you will increase the likelihood of a successful transfer of your credits and a smooth integration into your studies at Fulbright University Vietnam. Remember that the transfer process is ultimately managed by Fulbright's Office of the Registrar, so maintaining open communication with them is key.

### Question 29: Are 100-level and 200-level courses that are not explicitly categorized as E1, E2, E3, or E4 also excluded from fulfilling the Exploratory requirement?  
### Answer: 

Yes, according to the given contexts, only courses explicitly chosen from the categories E1 (Arts and Humanities), E2 (Social Sciences), and STEM (Science, Technology, Engineering, and Mathematics) can fulfill the Exploratory requirement. Courses that are not explicitly categorized as E1, E2, E3, or E4 are not mentioned as being eligible to fulfill this requirement. Therefore, 100-level and 200-level courses that are not explicitly categorized are excluded from fulfilling the Exploratory requirement. The text specifically states that students need to complete 8 credits (2 courses) chosen from each of the three categories (E1, E2, and STEM), and no other courses are mentioned as being eligible.

### Question 30: I am a Co25 student pursuing a Computer Science major, can I choose to follow either the old flowchart or the new one?  
### Answer: 

As a Co25 student pursuing a Computer Science major, you would typically follow the new curriculum structure, assuming the contexts provided represent a recent update to the program. The new structure outlines foundational, intermediate, and advanced courses, along with elective options and a capstone project to graduate with honors or additional courses to graduate without honors. This structure is designed to provide a comprehensive education in computer science, covering fundamental concepts, systems, applications, and emerging areas such as artificial intelligence, data science, and business analytics.

The old flowchart, if it exists, might have been based on a previous curriculum that did not include as much flexibility in elective choices, a structured progression through foundational, intermediate, and advanced courses, or the specific concentrations mentioned. However, without specific details about the old flowchart, it's challenging to provide a direct comparison.

Given the contexts provided, the new curriculum seems to offer a more structured and comprehensive approach to learning computer science, with a clear path from foundational knowledge to specialization and application. It also emphasizes practical experience through projects and the opportunity to concentrate in specific areas of interest.

To proceed, you should refer to the official academic calendar, course catalog, or departmental guidelines for your specific institution. These resources will provide the most accurate and up-to-date information on the curriculum options available to you as a Co25 student. It's also a good idea to consult with an academic advisor who can help tailor your educational path according to your interests and career goals within the framework of the current curriculum.

### Question 31: If I withdraw from a course after the 4th week of the semester, will I be deducted 4 credits of financial aid?  
### Answer: 

No, withdrawing from a course after the 4th week of the semester will not necessarily result in a deduction of 4 credits of financial aid. According to the provided context, if a student withdraws from a course beyond the halfway point (end of week 4 for an 8-week course or end of week 8 for a full-term course), they automatically receive an 'F' grade, which will affect their GPA. However, the financial aid implications specifically mention that no reduction in tuition is granted to students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. This means that if a student is registered for more than 12 credits and withdraws from a course after the 4th week, they will not have their financial aid reduced by 4 credits. The tuition credit policy and financial aid adjustments are separate and are governed by the "Fees & Charges Guide" for undergraduate students, which would provide more detailed information on how tuition credits are calculated in the case of approved withdrawals. Therefore, the withdrawal after the 4th week does not automatically equate to a 4-credit deduction from financial aid; it depends on the student's overall credit load and the specific policies outlined in the institution's financial aid and tuition policies.

### Question 33: Can I take 160 credits in 4 years given that I pay for the full tuition fee?  
### Answer: 

The minimum number of credits required to graduate with an undergraduate degree from Fulbright is 128 credit hours. The typical full-time course load is 16 credit hours per term or 4 courses of 4 credit hours each. To make standard progress toward completing the degree, students are required to register for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 20 credit hours per term. Therefore, in four years (8 terms), a student can take a maximum of 8 * 20 = 160 credit hours. However, it is important to note that while it is possible to accumulate 160 credits in four years, it is not necessarily advisable or required. The standard path is to take 16 credits per term, which totals 128 credits over four years, fulfilling the minimum requirement for graduation. Taking more than the standard load may result in additional financial burden without necessarily accelerating the completion of the degree. Additionally, while you can pay for the full tuition fee, exceeding the standard credit load may lead to increased costs for additional courses, books, and other educational expenses. It is also important to consider the academic workload and potential impact on your academic performance and well-being. Therefore, while it is technically possible to take 160 credits in four years, it is not necessarily the most efficient or financially prudent approach.

Attempted credits are the credit hours students enroll in and are recorded in the transcript, regardless of whether they complete the courses or pass them. This includes all courses for which a student receives a grade, including withdrawals (W), incompletes (I), repeats (RP), and failures (F). See section 4 for more details on different types of grades. To graduate with an undergraduate degree from Fulbright, a student must earn a minimum of 128 credit hours.  The typical full-time course load is 16 credit hours per term or 4 courses of 4 credit hours each. In order to make standard progress toward completing the degree, students are required to register for no fewer than 12 credit hours and no more than 20 credit hours per term. ### Question 34: What are the requirements for mathematics courses to pursue Human-Centered Engineering major?  
### Answer: 

The mathematics courses are part of the Foundation Engineering courses, which are designed specifically to bridge engineering practice, mathematics, and basic sciences. These courses only require the core engineering course as the prerequisite, if any, making them accessible to undetermined-major students to pursue courses of interest. Once students declare their major or minor in Engineering, they will gain more specialized knowledge and skills by taking more Engineering Foundation courses and Engineering Advanced courses.

### Question 35: How is the grade of a replaced course displayed on the transcript? Will it replace the old one or create a new entry in the transcript?  
### Answer: 

The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript.  

<details>

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 10]

A higher grade will be kept and used in the GPA calculation. The lower grade will be overwritten by the notation “RP”, meaning “repeated”, and remain on the transcript. Credits of the repeated course are counted toward the total attempted credits. A student may not retake the same course for the third time unless he/she failed the second time or if a specific grade is needed to meet the major requirements. ### Question 36: What are the courses that can replace the capstone for a given major?  
### Answer: 

If students choose not to do a capstone, they could replace it with 8 credits from IS courses, including at least 4 credits of 300-level courses. For other majors, students should consult with their prospective advisor(s) early in their junior year to determine the suitability and feasibility of their proposed project. The total number of earned credits must be at least 128. Electives enable students to explore different subjects across the curriculum to build a broad and diverse background or to pursue more specialized studies within their major. Alternatively, students may use electives to fulfill requirements for another minor or major.

The capstone may also draw from relevant extra-curricular activities and practical experience to support the research and realization of the project. The capstone should evidence competency in a primary disciplinary specialization (optionally supported by secondary and tertiary disciplinary areas). In the Art & Media Studies major, the capstone could take the form of a thesis, an art work, or a film. For other kinds of projects, please consult with your prospective advisor(s) early in your junior year to determine the suitability and feasibility of your proposed project. ### Question 37: Is it possible to graduate with double majors in Human-Centered Engineering and Applied Mathematics? If yes, please provide a sample study plan.  
### Answer: 

It is possible to graduate with double majors in Human-Centered Engineering and Applied Mathematics, but it would require careful planning and coordination due to the extensive course requirements for each major. Here is a sample study plan:

**Year 1:**
- **Fall Semester:**
  - General Education Core Courses (8 credits)
  - Introduction to Calculus (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Introduction to Engineering Design (4 credits) [Human-Centered Engineering]

- **Spring Semester:**
  - General Education Exploratory Courses (4 credits)
  - Linear Algebra (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Introduction to Computer Science (4 credits) [Human-Centered Engineering]

**Year 2:**
- **Fall Semester:**
  - Multivariable Calculus (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Introduction to Statistics (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Introduction to Human-Centered Design (4 credits) [Human-Centered Engineering]

- **Spring Semester:**
  - Ideas in Mathematics/Advanced Mathematical Writing (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Intermediate Course 1 (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Intermediate Course 2 (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Human-Centered Engineering Elective (4 credits)

**Year 3:**
- **Fall Semester:**
  - Pure Mathematics Course (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Advanced Course 1 in Applied Mathematics (4 credits)
  - Human-Centered Engineering Elective (4 credits)

- **Spring Semester:**
  - Advanced Course 2 in Applied Mathematics (4 credits)
  - Applied Elective Course 1 (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Human-Centered Engineering Capstone Project (4 credits)

**Year 4:**
- **Fall Semester:**
  - Research Seminar (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Applied Elective Course 2 (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Human-Centered Engineering Elective (4 credits)

- **Spring Semester:**
  - Capstone I (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Capstone II (4 credits) [Applied Mathematics]
  - Human-Centered Engineering Senior Design Project (4 credits)

**Experiential Learning and Electives:**
- Throughout the program, students should aim to complete their Experiential Learning requirements (4-12 credits) and additional electives as needed to meet the total credit requirements for both majors.

**Notes:**
- This plan assumes that students are able to take the necessary courses concurrently and that there are no scheduling conflicts between the two majors.
- Students should consult with academic advisors in both departments to ensure that all requirements are met and to adjust the plan as necessary.
- The plan may need adjustments based on course availability, prerequisites, and any changes to the curriculum.

This plan is a general guideline and should be tailored to the specific requirements and offerings of the institution. It is crucial to maintain a balance between the foundational and advanced courses required by each major while also ensuring that the experiential learning and capstone requirements are fulfilled.

### Question 38: Are the 4-credit EL counted towards the credits covered by financial aid?  
### Answer: 

No, the Experiential Learning (EL) credits are not specifically mentioned as being covered by financial aid in the provided contexts. The contexts discuss the requirements and structure of the EL program, including the maximum and minimum credits that must be completed, but they do not address financial aid coverage. Typically, financial aid covers tuition and fees, and sometimes room and board, but specific policies regarding the coverage of experiential learning credits would need to be verified with the financial aid office at Fulbright University Vietnam or through the university's official financial aid documentation. Students should consult with the financial aid office to understand the specifics of how their financial aid package applies to experiential learning credits.

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
tagged. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. 1 In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors. These courses are usually not cross-listed with the minors and are referred to as 'tagged' by these minors. 13.4  Experiential Learning Requirements Experiential Learning can count toward elective or major requirements (upon advisor approval). Fulbright’s Experiential Learning Program (ELP) exists to create, standardize, and facilitate academic credit-bearing experiential learning experiences. All full-time, undergraduate, degree- seeking students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement. Current ELP streams are housed within Academic Affairs (AA), Career Services (CS), and Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation

count toward elective or major requirements (upon advisor approval). Fulbright’s Experiential Learning Program (ELP) exists to create, standardize, and facilitate academic credit-bearing experiential learning experiences. All full-time, undergraduate, degree- seeking students must complete at least 04 credits and a maximum of 12 credits under the ELP as a graduation requirement. Current ELP streams are housed within Academic Affairs (AA), Career Services (CS), and Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation (CEI), where each team is responsible for administrative and academic duties of their respective stream(s). The ELP operates within Fulbright’s undergraduate program’s standard operating procedures and is developed in consultation with undergraduate program leadership and faculty. For more details, please refer here https://onestop.fulbright.edu.vn/s/article/Experiential-Learning- Program 13.5  Electives To complete the degree, students are free to take elective courses from the Course Catalogue. ### Question 39: What are the exchange policies of Fulbright?  
### Answer: 

Fulbright has specific policies regarding course registration, changes, drops, and withdrawals. The registration process involves formally recording a student's enrollment in a course by the Office of the Registrar, with the student agreeing to abide by Fulbright's bylaws, policies, codes, and procedures. Students cannot register for courses in the forthcoming term if they are not in "Active" status and/or have an outstanding financial balance on their account.

The course change/add/drop period lasts for two weeks from the start of each term, from the beginning of Week 1 to 4:00 PM on Friday of Week 2. Students can freely add/drop courses during this time, except for Core courses. Students are responsible for maintaining full-time enrollment status by registering for a minimum of 12 credits per term.

Course withdrawal policies are also in place, with two withdrawal periods per term. The first period allows students to withdraw from a course without consequence from 4:00 PM Friday of Week 2 to 4:00 PM Friday of Week 4. The second period allows for withdrawal from 4:00 PM Friday of Week 4 to 4:00 PM Friday of Week 8, with attempted credits and a "W" grade recorded on the transcript.

For exceptional circumstances, tuition credit may be considered upon approval from course instructors, the student's academic/major advisor, and the Registrar's Office, following the "Fees & Charges Guide" for undergraduate students. However, no reduction in tuition is granted to students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. Students should also be aware that course withdrawals may affect their financial aid eligibility if they result in fewer than the minimum credits for full-time enrollment.

The waitlist policy allows students to join a waitlist for courses that are full. Should space become available, students will be enrolled in the course in order of their appearance on the waitlist during the registration or Add/Drop period. When a waitlisted student is added to a course, it is the responsibility of the student to ensure they are still eligible to register for the course and have met all prerequisites.

No reduction in tuition is granted to students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. ### Question 40: What actions are considered plagiarism? Are AI-generated works counted?  
### Answer: 

Plagiarism involves presenting someone else's work, thoughts, or ideas as one's own without proper attribution. This includes but is not limited to copying text, ideas, images, or data from other sources without acknowledging the original author or source. In the context of AI-generated works, Fulbright University Vietnam's Code of Academic Integrity requires comprehensive documentation of AI usage in creative projects that incorporate generative AI. This documentation must include the objective and scope of AI usage, the tools and platforms employed, the process and methodology of AI incorporation, and how ethical and copyright issues are addressed. This means that while AI-generated content is permissible, it must be clearly distinguished from original work and properly documented to avoid being considered plagiarism. The university's regulations emphasize transparency and the acknowledgment of AI's role in the creative process to maintain academic integrity. Therefore, AI-generated works are not inherently considered plagiarism if they are properly documented and integrated into the project with clear attribution to the AI's contributions. However, failing to document AI usage or attempting to pass off AI-generated content as original work would be considered a violation of academic integrity.

Creative projects that incorporate generative AI must include comprehensive documentation covering every aspect of AI usage. ### Question 41: Why is the graduation requirement 128 credits, but the major requirement is only 48 credits?  
### Answer: 

The graduation requirement of 128 credits is the total number of earned credits that students need to complete their degree. This includes all courses they take, including major requirements, electives, and any other requirements such as exploratory courses or experiential learning. The major requirement of 56 credits (which includes specific courses like Introduction to Programming, Data Structures & Algorithms, and Computer Organization) is part of the overall 128 credits. The major requirement ensures that students have a strong foundation in their chosen field, while the remaining credits are filled with electives and other requirements to provide a well-rounded education. The breakdown allows students to explore various subjects (through electives) and also to delve deeply into their major field (through the major requirements). The exploratory requirements (8 credits for Class of 2023, 16 credits for Class of 2024, and 32 credits for Classes of 2025, 2026, and 2027) are additional to the major requirements and contribute to the total 128 credits. The mention of 48 credits as a potential misunderstanding, as the correct major requirement is 56 credits, not 48. The explanation provided is based on the given contexts and does not reference any irrelevant information.

### Question 42: Explain the cross-listed mechanism between courses.  
### Answer: 

The cross-listed mechanism between courses allows students to use up to 8 credits (two courses) to satisfy both their major and minor requirements. This means that if a course is cross-listed between a major and a minor, a student can count those credits toward both sets of requirements. For instance, if a student is majoring in subjects A and B and minoring in subject C, they can take courses that are cross-listed between A and B, B and C, and A and C, up to a total of 8 credits. This rule applies per each pair of majors or minors, meaning the total number of double-counted credits or courses is not restricted by this 8-credit limit. However, this rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged by the minors. For example, if major A requires certain courses from major B that are not cross-listed, these courses cannot be counted toward the 8-credit limit. Cross-listed courses are typically those that are shared between different departments or disciplines, and for interdisciplinary minors, the courses are often referred to as 'tagged' by these minors. This mechanism allows for flexibility in course selection and can help students efficiently meet their academic requirements.  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
and a minor) share cross- listed or tagged1 courses, up to 8 credits (two courses) can be used to satisfy both sets of requirements. This limit is per each pair of majors (or major and minor) and does not restrict the total number of double-counted credits or courses a student can claim. For example, a student can major in both A and B and minor in C with a total of 6 double-counted courses: two in A and B, two in B and C, and two in A and C. This rule does not apply to courses that are not cross-listed or tagged. For instance, if major A requires certain courses from major B for out-of-area foundation, exploration, or applications of the major, and these courses are not cross-listed between A and B, they do not count toward the 8-credit limit. 1 In interdisciplinary minors, the requirements mainly consist of courses from other majors. ### Question 43: When is the graduation ceremony for Co25?  
### Answer: 

The graduation ceremony for CO 2025 students would typically be scheduled after they have completed all their coursework, including the Capstone Project and its defense, and have met all other academic requirements. The exact timing of the graduation ceremony can vary depending on the university's academic calendar and other factors. It is usually held at the end of the academic year, typically in May or June for most universities in the United States. However, the specific date and details would be communicated to the students well in advance by the university's academic office or the department of International Studies (IS). Students should regularly check their university's official communications and academic calendar for the most accurate information regarding the graduation ceremony.

### Question 44: How does the core quest course selection work to fulfill core credits?  
### Answer: 

Class of 2027 students and earlier need to complete five core courses (20 credits). These core courses are: • Global Humanities (4 credits) • Modern Vietnamese Culture and Society (4 credits) • Quantitative Reasoning for a Digital Age (4 credits) • Scientific Inquiry (4 credits) • Design and Systems Thinking (4 credits).  

<details>  
<summary>References</summary>  

[Source: Academic-Policy_V5.1.pdf, Page: 20]
Class of 2027 students and earlier need to complete five core courses (20 credits). No course can be taken to fulfill more than one category. At most, 8 exploratory credits can be taken as Pass/No Pass. ### Question 45: Can I take a 300-level course to fulfill a 200-level major requirement?  
### Answer: 

No, according to the given context, students are required to take six (06) 200-level intermediate courses after taking Foundation courses. The requirement specifies that students must take 200-level courses, not 300-level courses, to fulfill the 200-level major requirement. The important note also states that until the university can offer a robust list of 200 and 300-Level Courses, students can satisfy the 48 Credits with any combination of 200 and 300 Level Courses, but they should be guided by the requirement to select 200-level courses where possible. Therefore, taking a 300-level course to fulfill a 200-level requirement would not be in accordance with the stated requirements.

### Question 46: If I take a course at another university and want to transfer the credits to Fulbright, but Fulbright doesn’t have that major code, yet the course is related to a major at Fulbright, can I count it toward that major?  
### Answer: 

No, if Fulbright does not have an equivalent course code for a course taken at another university, even if the course is related to a major at Fulbright, it cannot be counted toward that major. Fulbright's transfer credit policy requires that courses be equivalent to those offered at Fulbright, as determined by course curriculum, learning outcomes, content, and assessments. To apply for credit transfer, students must submit an Application for Credit Transfer along with all the required information about the course to the Office of the Registrar. Fulbright will evaluate the transferable credit after a student enrolls, and an official transcript with a seal from the issuing institution must be sent directly to the Office of the Registrar. Students should ensure that the course they wish to transfer is equivalent to a course at Fulbright before applying for transfer credit.

That is, to earn a Fulbright University Vietnam undergraduate degree, a student must earn at least 64 credit hours from courses delivered by Fulbright. ### Question 47: I have 112 credits from Fulbright and 16 credits from EL. Can I graduate?  
### Answer: 

To graduate with an undergraduate degree from Fulbright, a student must earn a minimum of 128 credit hours. You have 112 credits from Fulbright and 16 credits from EL, which totals 128 credits. Therefore, you can graduate. The Experiential Learning Program (ELP) credits count towards the graduation requirement, so you have met the necessary credit hour requirement. The references provided are relevant to the question, particularly the section on credit requirements for graduation. The information about ELP credits being a graduation requirement is also pertinent, confirming that your total credits (128) meet the university's standard for an undergraduate degree. The details about electives and capstone pathways are not directly relevant to your question about whether you can graduate based on the total number of credits earned.

### Question 48: I have received an offer to attend an international conference but lack funding. What school funds can I apply for?  
### Answer: 

Students may apply for limited funding for materials and resources for their projects. Policies on budgeting and purchasing will be provided. However, it's important to note that while this funding might cover some costs related to academic projects, it is generally not intended for personal travel expenses such as attending an international conference. For conference attendance, students typically need to explore other sources of funding such as scholarships, grants, or financial aid available from the university or external organizations. They might also consider reaching out to their academic advisor or the department for potential support or recommendations on how to apply for funding opportunities that align with the conference's theme and the student's academic pursuits. Additionally, students should check if their university has any specific funds or programs designed to support student travel for academic purposes, research presentations, or conferences. It's also worth looking into external scholarships and grants offered by professional organizations related to the student's field of study.

8. Resources and Support: Available Resources Students have access to labs, tools, and software relevant to their project work. They could be secondary advisors for Capstone projects, but not primary advisors or independent reviewers. They could also be invited to join the capstone panel. As either a secondary advisor or a member of the capstone panel, industry mentors could participate in grading. Funding and Material Procurement Projects will have access to limited funding for materials and resources. Policies on budgeting and purchasing will be provided.

### Question 50: In a regular semester, can I take only 2 courses? If so, how will my tuition fees be affected?  
### Answer: 

Yes, you can take only 2 courses, which amounts to 8 credits, as per the information provided. Regarding tuition fees, no reduction in tuition is granted to students whose course withdrawal results in fewer than 12 academic credits. Therefore, taking only 2 courses (8 credits) will not affect your tuition fees, assuming you remain enrolled in at least 12 credits throughout the semester. However, it's important to note that taking fewer than 12 credits may affect your financial aid eligibility. Always check with the financial aid office to understand how your specific situation might be impacted. Additionally, maintaining full-time enrollment status (12 credits) is crucial for various reasons, including eligibility for certain scholarships and grants, so it's advisable to plan your course load carefully.

### Question 51: Can I pursue 3 majors?  
### Answer: 

No, you cannot pursue three majors. The program described here limits students to a maximum of two majors, with the 8-credit Exploratory requirement independently applied to each major. This means if you have two majors, you would complete the Exploratory requirements separately for each major. The program does not explicitly allow for three majors, and the Exploratory requirement structure does not accommodate more than two majors. Therefore, pursuing three majors would not be possible under the described program. The Exploratory requirements are designed to ensure a broad educational foundation by requiring students to take courses in various disciplines outside their major(s), promoting a well-rounded education. The exemption for the Class of 2023 and the specific requirements for subsequent classes further illustrate the structure of the program, which is not designed to accommodate three majors simultaneously.

INTEGRATED SCIENCES: Description The Integrated Sciences major is a single, comprehensive program comprising a variety of fields, including Biology, Chemistry, and Environmental Science. Today’s most urgent scientific problems – from climate change to public health – require an integrated approach. Introducing multiple pathways in the natural science and emphasizing real-world experience, the Integrated Sciences major prepares students for careers as responsible and innovative scientific leaders and to seek interconnected solutions informed by several scientific disciplines. Students in the Integrated Sciences major will practice interdisciplinary and research-based approaches to scientific discovery, while also pursuing a specific concentration within the natural sciences: (1) Environmental Science and Sustainability, or (2) Biology and Health Science. The Integrated Sciences major provides a strong foundation for a career in industry as well as future graduate study. Learning Outcomes Students in the
